<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="Unbalanced scales.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/45px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png" width="45" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/68px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Unbalanced_scales.svg/90px-Unbalanced_scales.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="354" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>may be <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Neutral_point_of_view#Undue_weight" title="Wikipedia:Neutral point of view">unbalanced</a> towards certain viewpoints</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit">improve the article</a> by adding information on neglected viewpoints, or discuss the issue on the <a href="/wiki/Talk:Pope_Alexander_VI" title="Talk:Pope Alexander VI">talk page</a>.</span> <small><i>(March 2017)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg/220px-Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg" width="220" height="259" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg/330px-Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg/440px-Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="510" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Alexander_VI_-_Pinturicchio_detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Detail of fresco <i>Resurrection</i> in the Borgia Apartments, showing Alexander VI humbly in prayer<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[57]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Before the death of Alexander VI, Rome was filled with political instability. A famous quote by him stated the following "Never have mercy for those who won't help themselves." This reflected clearly the legacy of the Borgia Pope.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Following the death of Alexander VI, Julius II said on the day of his election: "I will not live in the same rooms as the Borgias lived. He desecrated the Holy Church as none before. "<sup id="cite_ref-NC2_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC2-74">[58]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Borgia_Apartments" title="Borgia Apartments">Borgias' apartments</a> remained sealed until the 19th century.<sup id="cite_ref-NC2_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NC2-74">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sometimes overlooked is the fact that Alexander VI set about reforms of the increasingly irresponsible Curia. He put together a group of his most pious cardinals in order to move the process along. Planned reforms included new rules on the sale of Church property, the limiting of cardinals to one bishopric, and stricter moral codes for clergy.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[Note 17]</a></sup> Had he stayed in office longer, the pontiff might have had more success in the enactment of these reforms.</p>
<p>Alexander VI was known for his patronage of the arts, and in his days a new architectural era was initiated in Rome with the coming of <a href="/wiki/Donato_Bramante" title="Donato Bramante">Bramante</a>. <a href="/wiki/Raffaello_Santi" class="mw-redirect" title="Raffaello Santi">Raphael</a>, <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">Michelangelo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pinturicchio" title="Pinturicchio">Pinturicchio</a> all worked for him.<sup id="cite_ref-Villari_17-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Villari-17">[14]</a></sup> He commissioned Pinturicchio to lavishly paint a suite of rooms in the Apostolic Palace in the Vatican, which are today known as the <a href="/wiki/Borgia_Apartments" title="Borgia Apartments">Borgia Apartments</a>. He took a great interest in theatrics, and he even had the <a href="/wiki/Menaechmi" title="Menaechmi">Menaechmi</a> performed in his apartments.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[Note 18]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to the arts, Alexander VI also encouraged the development of education. In 1495, he issued a <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> at the request of <a href="/wiki/William_Elphinstone" title="William Elphinstone">William Elphinstone</a>, Bishop of Aberdeen, and <a href="/wiki/James_IV_of_Scotland" title="James IV of Scotland">King James IV of Scotland</a>, founding <a href="/wiki/Kings_College,_Aberdeen" class="mw-redirect" title="Kings College, Aberdeen">King's College, Aberdeen</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[61]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[62]</a></sup> King's College now forms an integral element of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Aberdeen" title="University of Aberdeen">University of Aberdeen</a>. Alexander VI also, in 1501, approved the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Valencia" title="University of Valencia">University of Valencia</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[63]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander VI, allegedly a <a href="/wiki/Marrano" title="Marrano">marrano</a> according to papal rival <a href="/wiki/Giuliano_della_Rovere" class="mw-redirect" title="Giuliano della Rovere">Giuliano della Rovere</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[65]</a></sup> distinguished himself by his relatively benign treatment of Jews. After the 1492 <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_the_Jews_from_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Expulsion of the Jews from Spain">expulsion of the Jews from Spain</a>, some 9,000 impoverished <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Jews" class="mw-redirect" title="Iberian Jews">Iberian Jews</a> arrived at the borders of the <a href="/wiki/Papal_States" title="Papal States">Papal States</a>. Alexander welcomed them into Rome, declaring that they were "permitted to lead their life, free from interference from Christians, to continue in their own rites, to gain wealth, and to enjoy many other privileges". He similarly allowed the immigration of <a href="/wiki/Expulsion_of_the_Jews_from_Portugal" class="mw-redirect" title="Expulsion of the Jews from Portugal">Jews expelled from Portugal</a> in 1497 and from Provence in 1498.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been noted that the alleged misdeeds of Alexander VI are similar in nature to those of other Renaissance princes, with the one exception being his position in the Church. As <a href="/wiki/Joseph_de_Maistre" title="Joseph de Maistre">De Maistre</a> said in his work <i>Du Pape</i>, "The latter are forgiven nothing, because everything is expected from them, wherefore the vices lightly passed over in a <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a> become most offensive and scandalous in an Alexander VI."<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bohuslav_Hasi%C5%A1tejnsk%C3%BD_z_Lobkovic" title="Bohuslav Hasištejnský z Lobkovic">Bohuslav Hasištejnský z Lobkovic</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bohemia" title="Bohemia">Bohemian</a> <a href="/wiki/Humanism" title="Humanism">humanist</a> poet (1461–1510) dedicated one of his Latin poems to Alexander:<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[68]</a></sup></p>
<table>
<tr>
<td>
<p><b>Epitaphium Alexandri Papae</b></p>
</td>
<td>
<p><b>Epitaph to Pope Alexander</b></p>
</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td>
<dl>
<dd>Cui tranquilla quies odio, cui proelia cordi</dd>
<dd>et rixa et caedes seditioque fuit,</dd>
<dd>mortuus hac recubat populis gaudentibus urna</dd>
<dd>pastor Alexander, maxima Roma, tuus.</dd>
<dd>Vos, Erebi proceres, vos caeli claudite portas</dd>
<dd>atque Animam vestris hanc prohibete locis.</dd>
<dd>In Styga nam veniens pacem turbabit Averni,</dd>
<dd>committet superos, si petat astra poli.</dd>
</dl>
</td>
<td>
<dl>
<dd>Who sacrificed quiet to hatred, with a warrior heart,</dd>
<dd>who did not stop at quarrels, struggles and slaughters,</dd>
<dd>is lying here in the coffin for all people to rejoice,</dd>
<dd>thy supreme pontiff Alexander, oh, capital Rome.</dd>
<dd>Ye prelates of <a href="/wiki/Erebus" title="Erebus">Erebus</a> and Heaven, close your doors</dd>
<dd>and prohibit the Soul from entering your sites.</dd>
<dd>He would disrupt the peace of <a href="/wiki/Styx" title="Styx">Styx</a> and disturb <a href="/wiki/Avernus" title="Avernus">Avernus</a>,</dd>
<dd>and vanquish the Saints, if he enters the sphere of stars.</dd>
</dl>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Despite <a href="/wiki/Julius_II" class="mw-redirect" title="Julius II">Julius II</a>'s hostility, the Roman barons and Romagna vicars were never again to be the same problem for the papacy and Julius' successes owe much to the foundations laid by the Borgias.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[69]</a></sup> Unlike Julius, Alexander never made war unless absolutely necessary, preferring negotiation and diplomacy.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p>Alexander Lee argues that the crimes attributed to the Borgias were exaggerated by contemporaries because they were outsiders expanding their holdings at the expense of the Italians, that they were Spaniards when it was felt that Spain had too much control on the Italian peninsula, and that after the death of Alexander the family lost its influence and therefore any incentive for anyone to defend them.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_popular_culture">In popular culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: In popular culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="40" data-file-height="40" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>appears to contain <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_an_indiscriminate_collection_of_information" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">trivial, minor, or unrelated</a> references to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:%22In_popular_culture%22_content" title="Wikipedia:&quot;In popular culture&quot; content">popular culture</a></b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Trivia_sections" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Trivia sections">reorganize this content</a> to explain the subject's impact on popular culture rather than simply listing appearances; <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Editing_policy#Try_to_fix_problems" title="Wikipedia:Editing policy">add references</a> to <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">reliable sources</a> if possible, otherwise delete it.</span> <small><i>(September 2017)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Books">Books</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Books">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>The contemporary politician, political theorist and author <a href="/wiki/Niccol%C3%B2_Machiavelli" title="Niccolò Machiavelli">Niccolò Machiavelli</a> wrote his book of power politics <i><a href="/wiki/The_Prince" title="The Prince">The Prince</a></i> in 1513, in which he refers to Alexander VI as an astute politician who did much to strengthen the power of the Church.<sup id="cite_ref-Prince_XI_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prince_XI-90">[72]</a></sup> "Alexander VI, more than any other pontiff who has ever lived, showed how much a pope could achieve with money and armed force. ... Although his aim was the aggrandizement of the duke [his son Cesare], not of the Church, nonetheless what he did increased the greatness of the Church; and after his death ... the Church inherited the fruits of his labours. Then came Pope Julius [II]. He found the Church already great ... as a result of Alexander's vigour."<sup id="cite_ref-Prince_XI_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Prince_XI-90">[72]</a></sup></li>
<li>E. R. Chamberlin's 1969 book <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bad_Popes" title="The Bad Popes">The Bad Popes</a></i> documented the lives of eight of the most controversial popes, including Alexander.</li>
<li>Alexander, Cesare and Lucrezia play key roles in <a href="/wiki/Cecelia_Holland" title="Cecelia Holland">Cecelia Holland</a>'s 1979 historical novel <i><a href="/wiki/City_of_God_(Holland_novel)" title="City of God (Holland novel)">City of God: A Novel of the Borgias</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Maclaine_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Maclaine-91">[73]</a></sup></li>
<li>Alexander is one of six Popes of the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> era profiled unfavorably by historian <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Tuchman" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbara Tuchman">Barbara Tuchman</a> in <i>The March of Folly</i> (1984).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frederick_Rolfe" title="Frederick Rolfe">Frederick Rolfe</a> ("Baron Corvo") wrote <i><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.scribd.com/doc/115669189/Chronicles-of-the-House-of-Borgia">Chronicles of the House of Borgia</a></i>, a <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">revisionist</a> account in which he argued that the Borgia family was unjustly maligned and that the accounts of poisoning were a myth.</li>
<li>Alexander VI and his family are the subjects of <a href="/wiki/Mario_Puzo" title="Mario Puzo">Mario Puzo</a>'s final novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Family_(Mario_Puzo_novel)" class="mw-redirect" title="The Family (Mario Puzo novel)">The Family</a></i> (2001), as well as <a href="/wiki/Robert_Rankin" title="Robert Rankin">Robert Rankin</a>'s humorous and fictionalized novel <i><a href="/wiki/The_Antipope" title="The Antipope">The Antipope</a></i>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Borgia_Bride" title="The Borgia Bride">The Borgia Bride</a></i> (2005) is a historical fiction by <a href="/wiki/Jeanne_Kalogridis" title="Jeanne Kalogridis">Jeanne Kalogridis</a>, told from the perspective of Sancha of <a href="/wiki/Aragon" title="Aragon">Aragon</a>, married to the Pope's youngest son <a href="/wiki/Gioffre_Borgia" title="Gioffre Borgia">Gioffre Borgia</a>.</li>
<li>In March 2005, <i><a href="/wiki/Heavy_Metal_(magazine)" title="Heavy Metal (magazine)">Heavy Metal</a></i> published the first of a three-part graphic novel biography of Alexander VI entitled <i>Borgia</i>, written by <a href="/wiki/Alexandro_Jodorowsky" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexandro Jodorowsky">Alexandro Jodorowsky</a> with art by <a href="/wiki/Milo_Manara" title="Milo Manara">Milo Manara</a>. The story focuses mostly on the sexual indiscretions and acts of violent backstabbery carried out by the corrupt papal figure. The second part was released in July 2006 and the third in July 2009.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gregory_Maguire" title="Gregory Maguire">Gregory Maguire</a> makes strong references to Alexander VI and specifically his daughter in the 2003 novel, <i><a href="/wiki/Mirror,_Mirror_(novel)" title="Mirror, Mirror (novel)">Mirror, Mirror</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[74]</a></sup></li>
<li>Spanish author <a href="/wiki/Javier_Sierra" title="Javier Sierra">Javier Sierra</a> writes of Pope Alexander VI in his novel, <i>The Secret Supper</i>.</li>
<li>French author <a href="/wiki/Alexandre_Dumas" title="Alexandre Dumas">Alexandre Dumas</a>' <i><a href="/wiki/The_Count_of_Monte_Cristo" title="The Count of Monte Cristo">The Count of Monte Cristo</a></i> mentions murder of Cardinal Spada by Alexander VI and his son. This is told by Abbé Faria to Edmond Dantes in the prison in relation to a treasure belonging to Cardinal Spada.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[75]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Alexander_Dumas" class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Dumas">Alexander Dumas</a> also chronicles the life of the Borgia family in his "<a href="/w/index.php?title=Celebrated_Crimes,_vol_1&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Celebrated Crimes, vol 1 (page does not exist)">Celebrated Crimes, vol 1</a>".</li>
<li>Italian authors <a href="/wiki/Rita_Monaldi" title="Rita Monaldi">Rita Monaldi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Sorti" title="Francesco Sorti">Francesco Sorti</a> depict a totally different image of Pope Alexander VI in <i>The Doubts of Salaì</i> (2007). They reference sources which quote Alexander as an integral, hard-working functionary in the Roman Catholic Church. His infamous reputation would be largely attributed to falsified documents and the slander of his opponents.</li>
<li>Pope Alexander's diplomatic correspondence and intrigues with the Ottoman Turks, as well as <a href="/wiki/Charles_VIII_of_France" title="Charles VIII of France">Charles VIII</a>'s invasion of Italy, are depicted in the historical novel <i>The Sultan's Helmsman</i>.</li>
<li><i>Blood &amp; Beauty</i> by <a href="/wiki/Sarah_Dunant" title="Sarah Dunant">Sarah Dunant</a> is a well-researched historical novel of the Borgia family, focused on documentable incidents, with a particular focus on Lucrezia.</li>
<li>The introduction to <i>The Bad Catholic's Guide to Good Living</i>, by John Zmirak and Denise Matychowiak, is attributed to Pope Alexander, writing in 2005 from "The Seventh Terrace of <a href="/wiki/Purgatory" title="Purgatory">Purgatory</a>". In a postscript to the introduction, "Alexander" requests additional prayers for the sake of himself and several other popes stuck in Purgatory.</li>
<li>Prominently depicted in the non-fiction account: <i>The Borgias: The Hidden History</i> (2013) by G. J. Meyer</li>
<li>Alexander VI as well as his descendants were prominently featured in <a href="/wiki/Hella_Haasse" title="Hella Haasse">Hella Haasse</a>'s <i>The Scarlet City</i>.</li>
<li>The poisoning theory of Alexander VI's death was mentioned in Chapter VII of the <a href="/wiki/Dornford_Yates" title="Dornford Yates">Dornford Yates</a> thriller <i>Safe Custody</i>, as part of a highly sensationalized accout of his career. It was repeated in Chapter XIV.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Plays">Plays</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Plays">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Barnabe_Barnes" title="Barnabe Barnes">Barnabe Barnes</a>' 1606 play <i><a href="/wiki/The_Devil%27s_Charter" title="The Devil's Charter">The Devil's Charter</a></i>, performed at the Globe by the King's Men, dramatizes the life of Pope Alexander VI and his daughter Lucretia Borgia. In Barnes' play Alexander sells his soul to the devil in exchange for the papacy. Lucretia binds, gags, and stabs her husband onstage and later dies poisoned by her own cosmetics.</li>
<li><i>Cesare and Lucrezia Borgia</i>, a play by Robert Lalonde<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[76]</a></sup></li>
<li><i>Caesar Borgia, son of Pope Alexander the Sixth: a tragedy acted at the <a href="/wiki/Duke%27s_Theatre_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Duke's Theatre Company">Duke's theatre</a> by Their Royal Highnesses servants</i> a 1679 play by <a href="/wiki/Nathaniel_Lee" title="Nathaniel Lee">Nathaniel Lee</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Dryden" title="John Dryden">John Dryden</a>, dramatizes the life of Pope Alexander VI and his son <a href="/wiki/Cesare_Borgia" title="Cesare Borgia">Cesare Borgia</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Showtime_(TV_network)" title="Showtime (TV network)">Showtime</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Borgias_(2011_TV_series)" title="The Borgias (2011 TV series)">The Borgias</a>'</i>s ending after its cancellation was quickly published by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Jordan" title="Neil Jordan">Neil Jordan</a> in an e-book screenplay format <sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[77]</a></sup> for fans of the show. <a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Borgia_Apocalypse:_The_Screenplay&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="The Borgia Apocalypse: The Screenplay (page does not exist)">The Borgia Apocalypse: The Screenplay</a> met with harsh criticism and disapproval by the show's fans due to its out-of-character portrayals and general incoherence.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Film">Film</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Film">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>In the 1922 German silent film, <i>Lucrezia Borgia</i>, Alexander VI is played by Albert Basserman.</li>
<li>Alexander is played by <a href="/wiki/Llu%C3%ADs_Homar" title="Lluís Homar">Lluís Homar</a> in the 2006 Spanish film, <i>Los Borgia</i>.</li>
<li>A young Roderic de Borgia during the 1458 Conclave is played by Manu Fullola in the 2006 Canadian movie <i><a href="/wiki/The_Conclave" title="The Conclave">The Conclave</a></i>.</li>
<li>In the 1935 French movie, <i>Lucrezia Borgia</i>, Alexander is portrayed by Roger Karl.</li>
<li>The last of <a href="/wiki/Walerian_Borowczyk" title="Walerian Borowczyk">Walerian Borowczyk</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Immoral_Tales_(film)" title="Immoral Tales (film)">Contes Immoraux</a></i> (Immoral Tales) shows Jacopo Berenizi as Alexander VI, enjoying incest with Lucrezia and Cesare while <a href="/wiki/Savonarola" class="mw-redirect" title="Savonarola">Savonarola</a> is arrested and burned.</li>
<li>In the series of short films <i><a href="/wiki/Assassin%27s_Creed:_Lineage" title="Assassin's Creed: Lineage">Assassin's Creed: Lineage</a></i>, Rodrigo Borgia starts a conspiracy to destroy the Medici dynasty. In the first short film, he hires some assassins to kill the Duke of Milano, Galeazo Maria Sforza. He is played by <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Tadros" title="Manuel Tadros">Manuel Tadros</a>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>The papacy of Alexander VI was dramatized in the 1981 <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> series <i><a href="/wiki/The_Borgias_(1981_TV_series)" title="The Borgias (1981 TV series)">The Borgias</a></i>, starring the veteran Italian actor <a href="/wiki/Adolfo_Celi" title="Adolfo Celi">Adolfo Celi</a> as Pope Alexander.</li>
<li>The Canadian <a href="/wiki/Sketch_comedy" title="Sketch comedy">sketch comedy</a> <i><a href="/wiki/History_Bites" title="History Bites">History Bites</a></i> parodied Pope Alexander VI by portraying him and his family as The Osborgias (done as a parody of <a href="/wiki/The_Osbournes" title="The Osbournes">The Osbournes</a>).</li>
<li>In the popular TV show, <i><a href="/wiki/Alias_(TV_series)" title="Alias (TV series)">Alias</a></i>, the character <a href="/wiki/Milo_Rambaldi" class="mw-redirect" title="Milo Rambaldi">Milo Rambaldi</a> was said to be Alexander VI's "chief architect."</li>
<li>French premium-pay TV <a href="/wiki/Canal%2B" title="Canal+">Canal+</a>, Atlantique Productions and EOS Entertainment broadcast the series <i><a href="/wiki/Borgia_(2011_TV_Series)" class="mw-redirect" title="Borgia (2011 TV Series)">Borgia</a></i> in 2011, recounting the infamous family's rise to power and subsequent domination of the Vatican. <a href="/wiki/John_Doman" title="John Doman">John Doman</a> stars as Rodrigo Borgia. A second season followed in 2013 and season 3 was produced in 2014.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Showtime_(TV_network)" title="Showtime (TV network)">Showtime</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Borgias_(2011_TV_series)" title="The Borgias (2011 TV series)">The Borgias</a></i> (2011 - 2013) was produced by <a href="/wiki/Neil_Jordan" title="Neil Jordan">Neil Jordan</a> and starred <a href="/wiki/Jeremy_Irons" title="Jeremy Irons">Jeremy Irons</a> as Pope Alexander VI. The show had three seasons but was cancelled by Showtime after the third season "due to costs", as it cost $3 million per episode.</li>
<li>In the 2012 season of the BBC children's series <i><a href="/wiki/Horrible_Histories_(2009_TV_series)" title="Horrible Histories (2009 TV series)">Horrible Histories</a></i>, Alexander VI was dramatized by actor <a href="/wiki/Jim_Howick" title="Jim Howick">Jim Howick</a>. The show parodied Pope Alexander as a mafia crime boss, and later as the father of an <i><a href="/wiki/The_Addams_Family_(1964_TV_series)" title="The Addams Family (1964 TV series)">Addams Family</a></i>-style dynasty of the Borgias. (The Addams Family theme song was also parodied, being renamed <i>The Borgia Family</i>.)</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Video_games">Video games</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Video games">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Assassin%27s_Creed_II" title="Assassin's Creed II">Assassin's Creed II</a></i> (2009), Rodrigo Borgia is the main antagonist of the game, secretly the Grand Master of the <a href="/wiki/Knights_Templar" title="Knights Templar">Knights Templar</a>. He is also depicted as an <a href="/wiki/Atheist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atheist">atheist</a>. The protagonist (<a href="/wiki/Ezio_Auditore_da_Firenze" title="Ezio Auditore da Firenze">Ezio Auditore da Firenze</a>) tried to kill Alexander VI before ascending the papacy, but let him go, knowing that killing him would not bring his father and brothers back. Ezio even went on so far as to learn of Rodrigo's atheism. His character in the game is voiced by and modeled on Canadian actor <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Tadros" title="Manuel Tadros">Manuel Tadros</a>.</li>
<li>In <i><a href="/wiki/Assassin%27s_Creed:_Brotherhood" title="Assassin's Creed: Brotherhood">Assassin's Creed: Brotherhood</a></i> (2010), Rodrigo Borgia has a smaller role than his son, Cesare Borgia, the game's main antagonist. He is killed by Cesare, who after becoming aware of his father's plot to assassinate him (due to Cesare's campaigning in Romagna resulting in declining Borgia influence in Rome) forces his own poisoned apple in his mouth.</li>
</ul>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Pope_Alexander_VI&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<table class="plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style" role="presentation">
<tr>
<td class="mbox-image">
<div style="width:52px"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/40px-Edit-clear.svg.png" width="40" height="40" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/60px-Edit-clear.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/Edit-clear.svg/80px-Edit-clear.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="48" data-file-height="48" /></div>
</td>
<td class="mbox-text"><span class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>contains <a href="/wiki/MOS:QUOTATIONS" class="mw-redirect" title="MOS:QUOTATIONS">too many or too-lengthy quotations</a> for an encyclopedic entry</b>. <span class="hide-when-compact">Please <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles">help improve the article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Editing" title="Help:Editing">editing it</a> to take facts from excessively quoted material and rewrite them as <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">sourced</a> original prose. Consider transferring direct quotations to <a href="/wiki/Wikiquote" title="Wikiquote">Wikiquote</a>.</span> <small><i>(September 2017)</i></small></span></td>
</tr>
</table>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: decimal;">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-3"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-3">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Pope Alexander VI only recognized four children as his: Cesare, Giovanni, Lucrezia, and Gioffre. Some, including Christopher Hibbert, recognize up to six more: Girolama (or Jeronima), Isabella, <a href="/wiki/Pier_Luigi_Borgia" class="mw-redirect" title="Pier Luigi Borgia">Pier Luigi</a> (or Pedro Luis), Bernardo, Ottaviano, and Laura.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Alexander was a jovial, far-sighted, moderate man, well-balanced in mind and body. Having lived nearly half a century in Rome, and having been for almost the whole of his life part of the ecclesiastical organization, he had a profound respect for all the interests of the Catholic Church, a respect greater than for his own life. He was prepared to compromise upon all purely human questions, but inflexible upon whatever concerned the rights of religion. He was the type of 'political priest', cautious and slow to act in the fact [sic] of the unforeseen, but brave to the point of heroism in defence of the great Institution whose direction had been entrusted to him."<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-16"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-16">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"He was also a natural orator, a pleasant conversationalist, an expert in Canon Law and Theology, and 'so familiar with Holy Writ that his speeches sparkled with well chosen texts from Sacred Scripture'. He never ceased to be a student: if not occupied by Divine service or Church affairs he would be reading books. He also wrote for the instruction of others. It is admitted even by his enemies that he was a protector and promoter of literature and the sciences."<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[13]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Doubtful, but possible. On a similar claim: "Without any solid evidence Giulia is said to have been the model for Pinturicchio's 'Virgin and Child' surrounded by angels in the Borgia Apartments of the Vatican."<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[16]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Next morning the absence of the Duke was noticed by his servants, and the Pontiff was informed. He was not too worried for, as Burchard says, Alexander jumped to the conclusion that his son had spent the night with some girl and preferred to avoid the indiscretion of leaving by day. It may be mentioned in passing that this touch, as with many others one comes across, hardly squares with the general view that the Pope, his family and those around him were without shame. Juan was a dissolute young man and not a churchman, yet Alexander presumed on a discretion more in keeping with later times."<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-31">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The fact that father and son [Alexander and Cesare] had collapsed on the same day inevitably aroused suspicions of foul play. It was pointed out that on the third [of August 1503] the two of them had dined with the recently appointed <a href="/wiki/Adriano_Castellesi" title="Adriano Castellesi">Cardinal Adriano Castellesi</a> in his nearby villa; the rumor rapidly spread around Rome that they had intended to poison their host but had inadvertently drunk the poisoned wine themselves. For some reason this mildly ridiculous story has survived and found its way into a number of serious histories; it ignores the fact that..they had no ascertainable motive to kill Castellesi."<sup id="cite_ref-poison1_30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-poison1-30">[25]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"We need now to digress a little to explain why the Pope should bestow his favours so generously on his own relatives. Let us take a look first at the political situation in Spain and in Italy. For centuries, Spain had been almost completely overridden by the Moors. The Spaniards had been trying to take back their country from the Moors for almost 800 years. By the middle of the 15th century, this reconquest was almost complete, but Spain was still a hodgepodge of competing principalities and, because of its constant state of warfare, still a very backward country. In Italy, on the other hand, the Renaissance, which had hardly begun in Spain, had reached its high point and the Italians in general did not look kindly on a citizen of this backward country being elevated to the highest post in the Church. Remember, too, that the Pope at the time, besides his spiritual powers, was a sovereign political power with large areas of the peninsula, nominally, at least, under his control. (see map) Italy was, politically, in a worse state than Spain. In the south, Naples was a fief of the Pope, but its ruler, King Ferrante, refused to acknowledge the Pope's authority. In the north of the peninsula, many small principalities vied for dominance and were often at war with one another, changing alliances as rapidly as opportunity invited. In the Papal States themselves, noble families, such as the Orsini and the Colonna, acted as petty tyrants in the cities and areas which they controlled, grinding down the people and constantly seeking to achieve their independence from their sovereign, the Pope. These Roman families even sought to control the Papacy itself. It was probably only because they could not agree on an Italian successor to Nicholas V that the elderly Callistus had been elected; one who, in all probability, would not live long. (Remember that, in our own times, John XXIII was supposed to have been elected for the same reason). Callistus III was acknowledged by all as religious and austere, though severely criticized for his largesse to his family. But he was surrounded by enemies both within the Church and among the rulers of Europe. When elected, he did what all leaders do, he surrounded himself with people whom he believed he could trust. A Spaniard in Italy, he was hard pressed to find such trustworthiness except from members of his own family; hence his patronage of them, though it is not to be denied that it was probably also for personal reasons."<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[30]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"As for her reputation, there is absolutely no evidence for the rumors of incest with one or more of her brothers – or indeed with her father – apart from that given by her first husband, Giovanni Sforza, during the divorce proceedings, during which several other baseless accusations were leveled in both directions."<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[31]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The holy year 1500 definitively ushered in the custom of opening a holy door on Christmas Eve and closing it the following year on Christmas Day. Alexander opened the first holy door in St. Peter's Basilica on Christmas Eve, 1499, and papal legates opened the doors in the other three patriarchal basilicas. For this occasion, Pope Alexander had a new opening created in the portico of St. Peter's and commissioned a door, made of marble, 3.5 meters high and 2.2 wide. It lasted until 1618 when another door was installed in the new basilica. The door, in turn, was replaced in 1950 by the bronze door, which is still in use. In a ceremony strikingly similar in many ways to today's ritual opening of a holy door, Alexander was carried in the gestatorial chair to the portico of St. Peter's. He and the members of his retinue, bearing long candles, processed to the holy door, as the choir intoned Psalm 118:19-20: "Open for me the gate of Yahweh, where the upright go in." The Pope knocked thrice on the door, it gave way (assisted from within by workers), and everyone then crossed the threshold to enter into a period of penance and reconciliation. Thus, Pope Alexander, a lover of pomp and ceremony, formalized the rite of opening a holy door and began a tradition that continues, with few variations, to this day. Similar rites were held at the other patriarchal basilicas. Alexander was also the first to institute a special rite for the closing of a holy door. On the feast of the Epiphany, January 6, 1501, two cardinals – one with a silver brick and the other with a gold one – symbolically began to seal the holy door. Basilica workers known as sanpietrini completed the task, which included placing small coins and medals, minted during the holy year, inside the wall."<sup id="cite_ref-holydoors_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-holydoors-40">[32]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-43"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-43">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"[A]ll the clergy of the city were invited to the opening of the [1500] Jubilee. The Pope himself performed this ceremony on Christmas Eve, 1499, having taken pains to settle all the details beforehand with his Master of Ceremonies. The ceremonial observed on these occasions was no modern invention, but, as the Bull of Indiction expressly says, was founded on ancient rites and full of symbolic meaning. According to Burchard, the crowd which assisted at these solemnities numbered 200,000 persons. Although this may be an exaggeration, still it is certain that, in spite of the troubles of the times and the insecurity in Rome itself, the numbers attending this Jubilee were very large."<sup id="cite_ref-rorate-jubilee_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rorate-jubilee-42">[33]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The permanent group of skilled workers and artisans, in every trade, who with their assistants take care of St. Peter's Basilica."<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[34]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-58"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-58">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"[T]here is every reason to believe," writes the <i><a href="/wiki/Dublin_Review_(Catholic_periodical)" title="Dublin Review (Catholic periodical)">Dublin Review</a>,</i> that Pope Alexander VI died "in sentiments of piety and devotion."<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[46]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-61"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-61">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The historical value of Bishop Celadoni's funeral oration is said to be immense: "On 16 Sept 1503 Burchardus records in his diary that Alexius Celadenus or Celadonius, bishop of Gallipoli, delivered a discourse to the cardinals about to enter into conclave for the election of a successor to Pope Alexander VI. <i>Et fuit tediosa et longa oratio</i>. Burchardus's most recent editor, Thuasne, states that this oration exists in manuscript in the Bibliothèque Nationale... He omits to observe that, granting that the discourse may have been too long for the cardinals, the longer the better for us, inasmuch as it <i>contains an account of Pope Alexander of almost unique value,</i> not merely as the judgment of a contemporary, but as delivered in public before an audience of contemporaries whose station in the church had brought them into almost daily intercourse with the deceased pope, and before whom <i>any serious misrepresentation would have been impossible</i>. It is incomprehensible how he should have failed to reprint <i>an historical testimony of such importance,</i> having it under his own eyes."<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[47]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[48]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-64"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-64">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Latin text: "Dum graviter aegrotaret, factorum conscientia punctus contrito dolentique animo ad lachrymas ut audio fusus, sacrosanctum communionis corpus sua sponte, dilutis prius diligentissima confessione peccatis, petierit, et alia sacramenta..."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[50]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-68"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-68">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Throughout the Middle Ages a 'tradition' or 'custom' involving pillaging was attached to the death and election of high-ranking prelates."<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[51]</a></sup> And as early as 633, "the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Council_of_Toledo" title="Fourth Council of Toledo">Fourth Council of Toledo</a> condemned the violence of the <a href="/wiki/Interregnum" title="Interregnum">interregnum</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[52]</a></sup> Nor were the Romans alone guilty of such misbehavior. In the eleventh century, <a href="/wiki/Peter_Damian" title="Peter Damian">Peter Damian</a>, writing to the clergy and people of <a href="/wiki/Osimo" title="Osimo">Osimo</a>, sharply reproved the "perverse and wholly detestable practice of certain people, who at the death of the bishop break in like enemies and rob his house, like thieves make off with his belongings, set fire to the homes on his estate, and with fierce and savage barbarity cut down his grape vines and orchards."<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">[53]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-71"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-71">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"In consequence of the simultaneous illness of both the Pope and his son, and the rapid decomposition of the body, which, considering the heat of the weather, was perfectly natural, the cry of poison was raised at once; but on the 19th of August the Mantuan Envoy writes that there was no sort of ground for supposing this."<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[55]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-76"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-76">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"As part of his proposed new reforms, Alexander now nominated a commission of six of the most pious cardinals, and less than two months later a draft Bull of Reformation had been prepared. The pope was banned from selling benefices and from transferring Church property to laypersons. As for the cardinals, who were to be drawn from all the nations, none should possess more than one bishopric; their households were limited to eighty people and thirty horses; they were banned from hunting, theaters, carnivals, and tournaments; and their funeral expenses were not to exceed 1,500 ducats. The lesser clergy were similarly reined in: they must refuse all bribes and put away their concubines."<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[59]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-78"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-78">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Under Alexander VI, the taste for theatrical representations made great progress. Plays, for the most part of an extremely objectionable character, were a prominent feature in all court festivities, and also in the Carnival amusements, in which Alexander took a great interest. In 1502 the Pope had the Menaechmi performed in his own apartments."<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[60]</a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-Burkle-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Burkle_9-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Burkle_9-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Burkle_9-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www2.fiu.edu/~mirandas/election-alexandervi.htm">"The Cardinals of the Holy Roman Church - Papal elections - XV Century"</a>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APope+Alexander+VI&amp;rft.btitle=The+Cardinals+of+the+Holy+Roman+Church+-+Papal+elections+-+XV+Century&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww2.fiu.edu%2F~mirandas%2Felection-alexandervi.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>

<li id="cite_note-Villari-17"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Villari_17-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Villari_17-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Villari_17-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Villari_17-3"><sup><i><b>d</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Villari_17-4"><sup><i><b>e</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Villari_17-5"><sup><i><b>f</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFVillari1911">Villari (1911)</a><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="This citation requires a reference to the specific page or range of pages in which the material appears. (May 2017)">page&#160;needed</span></a></i>]</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-18"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-18">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFWilliams2004">Williams (2004)</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=-mq7ctwMNdoC&amp;pg=PA70&amp;lpg=PA70#v=onepage&amp;q&amp;f=false">p. 70</a></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-19"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-19">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFde_la_B.C3.A9doy.C3.A8re1958">de la Bédoyère (1958)</a>, p.&#160;94</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFde_Rossa1989">de Rossa (1989)</a>, p.&#160;144</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-22"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-22">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMallett1981">Mallett (1981)</a>, pp.&#160;123–126</span></li>

<th scope="row" class="navbox-group" style="background-color: gold;width:1%">Church beginnings,<br />
<a href="/wiki/Great_Church" title="Great Church">Great Church</a></th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-even" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">Jesus</a></li>
